\;\xrightarrow[\phi\to-\infty]{}\; 0
\label{eq:transition_gradient_main}
\end{equation}
falls toward zero.

\paragraph{Resolution of the regime-ordering problem.}
This observation resolves a crucial difficulty that plagues
constant-threshold nonlinear models. If the transition scale were
a fixed constant, the nonlinear (MOND-like) regime would activate
at \emph{large} gradients (near masses) and the linear (Newtonian)
regime at \emph{small} gradients (far away) --- the opposite of
what is observed. With a field-dependent threshold:
\begin{itemize}
\item Near massive bodies ($\bar\phi > 0$, deep Lorentzian phase):
  $|\nabla\phi|_{\rm trans}$ is large, so the system stays in its
  linear screened branch.
\item On galactic outskirts ($\bar\phi \to 0^-$, near-critical):
  $|\nabla\phi|_{\rm trans} \to 0$, so even weak gradients
  activate the nonlinear branch.
\end{itemize}
This gives the physically correct ordering: GR near masses,
MOND-like dynamics in dilute regions.

The exact scaling of $L(\phi)$ is not yet fully derived from the
microscopic theory; accordingly, this step should be viewed as
EFT-supported rather than fully established. The essential
requirement is the hierarchy $L/K \to \infty$ near criticality,
not any specific exponent.


%%% -----------------------------------------------------------
\subsection{Critical infrared regime}
\label{subsec:lof_critical}

Once the perturbative gradient expansion breaks down, it is
natural to replace it by a critical infrared effective theory for
the dimensionless order field $\phi$. Defining
$Y_\phi \equiv (\nabla\phi)^2$, the deep-IR static free energy
can be written as
\begin{equation}
F_{\rm IR}[\phi]
= \int d^3x\left[
  \Lambda^4\,\mathcal{F}\!\left(\frac{Y_\phi}{Y_*}\right)
  + U_{\rm eff}(\phi;\rho)
\right].
\label{eq:fir_main}
\end{equation}

\paragraph{Scale-invariance argument.}
At a 3D scale-invariant fixed point, if $\phi$ is treated as
dimensionless ($[\phi] = 0$), then $[Y_\phi] = L^{-2}$ and
the free-energy density must have dimension $L^{-3}$.  For a
power-law $\mathcal{F} \sim Y_\phi^p$:
\begin{equation}
[Y_\phi^p] = L^{-2p} \;\stackrel{!}{=}\; L^{-3}
\quad\Longrightarrow\quad
\boxed{p = 3/2\,.}
\end{equation}
This is not a claim of absolute uniqueness among all conceivable
nonlocal or logarithmically corrected structures. Rather, it is
the unique simple local power-law form built only from $Y_\phi$
that is consistent with 3D scale invariance when $[\phi] = 0$.
(An anomalous dimension $\Delta_\phi \neq 0$ would shift the
exponent to $p = 3/[2(1+\Delta_\phi)]$; for a Lorentz-order
parameter this correction is expected to be small.)

\paragraph{Physical interpretation.}
The $Y_\phi^{3/2}$ form means that at criticality the ``cost''
of a gradient in $\phi$ grows faster than quadratic: the system
resists non-uniformity in the degree of Lorentzianness more
strongly than a linear theory would predict.  This nonlinear
stiffness is what produces the $1/r$ force law needed for flat
rotation curves.

\paragraph{Full interpolating equation.}
The complete static equation, valid from the Newtonian to the
deep-MOND regime, takes the AQUAL-like form
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
\nabla\cdot\left[
  \mu_\phi\!\left(
    \frac{|\nabla\phi|}{\phi_*(\phi)}
  \right)\nabla\phi
\right]
= \frac{4\pi\rho}{c^2}\,,
}
\label{eq:aqual_phi_main}
\end{equation}
with
\begin{equation}
\mu_\phi(x) \to 1 \quad (x \ll 1)\,,
\qquad
\mu_\phi(x) \to x \quad (x \gg 1)\,.
\label{eq:mu_limits_main}
\end{equation}
The key structural difference from standard MOND is that the
transition scale $\phi_*(\phi)$ depends on the local
Lorentz-order background. This field dependence is what makes
the nonlinear branch appear in dilute near-critical regions
rather than near dense sources.


%%% -----------------------------------------------------------
\subsection{Baryonic Tully--Fisher relation}
\label{subsec:lof_btfr}

In the weak-field Jordan-frame regime, the order field contributes
an additional acceleration
\begin{equation}
\mathbf{a}_\phi \;\simeq\; -\frac{c^2}{2}\,\nabla\phi\,.
\label{eq:aphi_main}
\end{equation}
In the deep-MOND branch ($\mu_\phi(x) \to x$), integrating
equation~\eqref{eq:aqual_phi_main} over a sphere of radius $r$
enclosing total baryonic mass $M$:
\begin{equation}
4\pi r^2\cdot\frac{|\phi'|^2}{\phi_*}
= \frac{4\pi M}{c^2}\,,
\qquad\Longrightarrow\qquad
|\phi'| = \sqrt{\frac{M\,\phi_*}{c^2}}\;\frac{1}{r}\,.
\end{equation}
The circular velocity satisfies $v^2/r = |a_\phi|
= (c^2/2)|\phi'|$, giving
\begin{equation}
v^2 = \frac{c^2}{2}\sqrt{\frac{M\,\phi_*}{c^2}}
= \frac{1}{2}\sqrt{c^2 M\,\phi_*}\,,
\end{equation}
and therefore
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
v^4 = G\,M\,g_\dagger(\phi_{\rm gal})\,,
\qquad
g_\dagger \;\propto\; c^2\,\phi_*(\phi_{\rm gal})\,.
}
\label{eq:btfr_main}
\end{equation}
The $\mathcal{O}(1)$ proportionality coefficient depends on the
normalisation of the source term in the $\phi$-equation (OP-new-2);
the observed value $g_\dagger \approx 1.2\times10^{-10}\,$m\,s$^{-2}$
implies a coefficient $\sim\!1/6$ relative to $cH_0$.
The exponent $v^4 \propto M$ is \emph{exact} within the deep-MOND
branch and reproduces the baryonic Tully--Fisher relation. It is
not imposed but follows algebraically from the $Y_\phi^{3/2}$
structure.

The correct status statement is the following: BTFR is a strict
consequence of the critical infrared EFT of the Lorentz-order
field, and that EFT is strongly motivated by ECT and by the
perturbative breakdown analysis, but the bridge from the
microscopic ECT condensate to the critical branch is not yet fully
closed.


%%% -----------------------------------------------------------
\subsection{Quasi-universality and environmental dependence of
$g_\dagger$}
\label{subsec:lof_environment}

In standard MOND the acceleration scale
$a_0 \approx 1.2\times10^{-10}\,$m\,s$^{-2}$ is treated as a
universal constant whose origin is unexplained. In the present
framework,
\begin{equation}
g_\dagger = g_\dagger(\phi_{\rm gal})\,,
\end{equation}
so the acceleration scale is controlled by the ambient degree of
Lorentzian order at galactic outskirts.

\paragraph{Quasi-universality.}
For most galaxies $\phi_{\rm gal}$ is set mainly by the
cosmological background and therefore does not vary strongly
from one system to another.  The observed near-universality of
the radial acceleration relation ($\sigma \approx 0.13\,$dex)
is therefore naturally accommodated.

\paragraph{Order-of-magnitude estimate.}
Dimensional reasoning indicates that the characteristic
galactic acceleration scale is naturally set by the
cosmological scale $cH_0$, up to an undetermined coefficient
of order unity.  More specifically,
from eq.~\eqref{eq:btfr_main}, $g_\dagger \propto c^2\phi_*$,
where $\phi_*$ is the field-dependent transition gradient
evaluated at $\phi \approx 0$ (the cosmological vacuum).

The transition gradient $\phi_* = K/L$ is a ratio of kinetic
coefficients whose natural scale is set by the Hubble rate $H_0$.
In the linear regime the $\phi$-field has a vacuum
mass $m_\phi(0) = \sqrt{U_0''(0)/K(0)}$.  If the cosmological
vacuum is near-critical, this mass is of order $H_0/c$ (the
lightest dynamical scale in the present-day universe).  Therefore:
\begin{equation}
g_\dagger \;\propto\; c\,H_0\,.
\label{eq:cH0_main}
\end{equation}
With $cH_0 \approx 7\times10^{-10}\,$m\,s$^{-2}$,
the observed value
$g_\dagger^{\rm obs} \approx 1.2\times10^{-10}\,$m\,s$^{-2}$
corresponds to a coefficient $\sim\!1/6$.
The structural result is that $g_\dagger$ is proportional to
$cH_0$; the $\mathcal{O}(1)$ coefficient depends on the
detailed form of $V(\phi)$ and $K(\phi)$ near $\phi = 0$
(OP-new-2).
The $cH_0$ scale itself arises from the near-criticality of the
cosmological vacuum, not from a fine-tuned coincidence.

\paragraph{Environmental modulation (prediction).}
ECT predicts a weak systematic dependence:
\begin{equation}
\frac{\delta g_\dagger}{g_\dagger}
\;\approx\; \gamma\,\delta\phi_{\rm env}\,.
\label{eq:env_prediction_main}
\end{equation}
Specifically:
\begin{itemize}
\item \textbf{Cluster galaxies}
  ($\rho_{\rm ambient} > \bar\rho_{\rm cosm}$):
  $\phi_{\rm gal}$ higher, $g_\dagger$ higher.
\item \textbf{Field galaxies}: $g_\dagger$ at the universal value.
\item \textbf{Void dwarfs}
  ($\rho_{\rm ambient} < \bar\rho_{\rm cosm}$):
  $g_\dagger$ lower.
\end{itemize}
At present the \emph{sign} of the environmental modulation is
more robust than its amplitude: denser ambient environments are
expected to increase the local degree of Lorentzian order and
hence raise $g_\dagger$, but the magnitude of this effect still
depends on the undetermined response coefficient $\gamma$ and
on the detailed form of the medium-corrected potential.

This is a qualitative distinction from standard MOND (where $a_0$
is a fundamental constant) and from $\Lambda$CDM (where there is
no characteristic acceleration scale).

\paragraph{Proposed observational test.}
Cross-matching rotation-curve samples (e.g.\ SPARC) with
large-scale-structure catalogues would allow extraction of
$g_\dagger$ for each galaxy and testing for a correlation with
local cosmic density.  A positive correlation would support ECT;
absence of correlation at the $\lesssim\!10\%$ level would
constrain the parameter $\gamma$.

An analogous redshift dependence
$g_\dagger = g_\dagger(z)$ is expected if the cosmological
degree of Lorentzianness evolves in time (OP-new-4).
In this sense the galactic acceleration scale is not merely
phenomenological but potentially linked to the cosmological
history of the vacuum order parameter.

\paragraph{Potential signatures beyond rotation curves.}
Because the scalar contribution acts through the
order-dependent effective metric, it may also induce
characteristic low-density modifications of gravitational
redshift and lensing.  A systematic derivation of the two
metric potentials (Bardeen $\Phi$ and $\Psi$) in the
Lorentz-order-field branch remains future work; however, the
existence of a non-trivial $\phi$-profile implies that the
lensing-to-dynamics ratio may differ from the GR value of
unity in dilute environments --- another testable signature.


%%% -----------------------------------------------------------
\subsection{Quasistatic effective kinetics and practical formulas}
\label{subsec:lof_practical}

The preceding subsections motivate the $\phi$-framework
conceptually.  Here we collect the minimal set of formulas
needed for numerical calculations, deriving them from the
effective kinetic function rather than postulating them.

\subsubsection{Effective kinetic function $\mathcal{F}(Y)$}

In the quasistatic regime the $\phi$-sector is described by
an effective action of the form
\begin{equation}
S_\phi^{\rm stat}
= \int d^3x\,\Bigl[
  \Lambda_\phi^4\,\mathcal{F}(Y)
  + U_{\rm eff}(\phi;\rho)
\Bigr]\,,
\qquad
Y \equiv \frac{(\nabla\phi)^2}{\phi_*^2}\,,
\label{eq:Sphi_stat}
\end{equation}
where $\phi_* = \phi_*(\phi_{\rm env})$ is the
field-dependent transition gradient and $\Lambda_\phi$ is an
energy scale.

The asymptotic behaviour of $\mathcal{F}(Y)$ is
\emph{constrained by the physics}:
\begin{itemize}
\item \textbf{Screened branch} ($Y \gg 1$, dense
  environments): $\mathcal{F}(Y) \to Y$.
  This recovers Newton/GR.
\item \textbf{Critical branch} ($Y \ll 1$, dilute outskirts):
  $\mathcal{F}(Y) \to \tfrac{2}{3}\,Y^{3/2}$.
  This is the unique 3D scale-invariant form
  (Section~\ref{subsec:lof_critical}).
\end{itemize}
These asymptotes are not postulated but follow from
screening (the first) and 3D critical scaling (the second).

\subsubsection{Interpolating function from $\mathcal{F}$}

Variation of~\eqref{eq:Sphi_stat} with respect to $\phi$
yields
\begin{equation}
\nabla\cdot\bigl[\mathcal{F}_Y(Y)\,\nabla\phi\bigr]
= \text{source}(\rho,\phi)\,,
\end{equation}
where $\mathcal{F}_Y \equiv d\mathcal{F}/dY$.
In spherical symmetry this reduces to an algebraic relation
between $g_{\rm obs}$ and $g_N$, controlled by the
\emph{derived} interpolating function
\begin{equation}
\mu_\phi(x) \equiv \mathcal{F}_Y(x^2)\,,
\qquad x = \frac{|\nabla\phi|}{\phi_*}\,.
\label{eq:mu_from_F}
\end{equation}
Thus $\mu_\phi$ is \emph{not} a primary postulate but a
consequence of the choice of $\mathcal{F}$.

\paragraph{Minimal closure.}
The simplest $\mathcal{F}(Y)$ that interpolates between the
two required asymptotes is
\begin{equation}
\mathcal{F}(Y) = \sqrt{Y(1+Y)} - \operatorname{arsinh}\sqrt{Y}\,,
\label{eq:F_closure}
\end{equation}
which gives
$\mu_\phi(x) = x/\sqrt{1+x^2}$.
This is the form used in the present exploratory fits.
It should be regarded as a \emph{minimal phenomenological
completion} of the two theory-motivated asymptotes, not as a
first-principles derivation of the full transition shape.

\paragraph{Generalised family.}
For future work a one-parameter generalisation is
$\mu_n(x) = x/(1+x^n)^{1/n}$, where $n$ controls the
transition sharpness ($n=2$ recovers the minimal closure).

\subsubsection{Summary of practical formulas}
\label{subsec:lof_formulas}

\noindent\textbf{(a) Environment-dependent critical scale:}
\begin{equation}
g_\dagger(\phi_{\rm env})
= g_{\dagger 0}\,e^{\gamma\phi_{\rm env}}\,.
\label{eq:gdagger_practical}
\end{equation}
Here $g_{\dagger 0} \approx 1.2\times10^{-10}\,$m\,s$^{-2}$
is calibrated to the observed RAR scale, and $\gamma$ is
the \emph{response coefficient} controlling how strongly the
environment shifts the critical threshold.  In the present
exploratory fits $\gamma$ is treated as a working parameter
(not derived from first principles); its value is determined
by the spread of the observed RAR scatter across different
environments.  A first-principles derivation of $\gamma$
from $V(\phi)$ and $K(\phi)$ is listed as OP-new-2.

\noindent\textbf{(b) Algebraic closure (spherical symmetry):}
\begin{equation}
\mu_\phi\!\left(\frac{g_{\rm obs}}{g_\dagger}\right)
g_{\rm obs} = g_N(r)\,,
\qquad
g_{\rm obs}^2
= \frac{g_N^2 + \sqrt{g_N^4 + 4\,g_N^2\,g_\dagger^2}}{2}\,.
\label{eq:practical_closure}
\end{equation}

\noindent\textbf{(c) Newtonian baryonic input:}
\begin{equation}
g_N(r) = g_{\rm disc}(r) + g_{\rm bulge}(r)
+ g_{\rm gas}(r)\,.
\end{equation}

\noindent\textbf{(d) Rotation curve:}
$v_c(r) = \sqrt{r\,g_{\rm obs}(r)}$.

\noindent\textbf{(e) Deep critical limit (BTFR):}
\begin{equation}
g_{\rm obs} \approx \sqrt{g_N\,g_\dagger}
\;\;\Longrightarrow\;\;
v_c^4 \approx G\,M_b\,g_\dagger(\phi_{\rm env})\,.
\end{equation}

\noindent\textbf{(f) RAR family:}
$g_{\rm obs} = \mathcal{G}(g_{\rm bar};\,g_\dagger(\phi_{\rm env}),\,n)$
--- a narrow family of curves, not a single universal line.

\noindent\textbf{(g) Next-stage full field solver:}
\begin{equation}
\frac{1}{r^2}\frac{d}{dr}\!\left[
r^2\,\mu_\phi\!\left(\frac{|\phi'|}{\phi_*(\phi)}\right)
\phi'\right]
= \frac{4\pi\rho(r)}{c^2}\,,
\qquad
\phi(r\to\infty) = \phi_{\rm env}\,.
\label{eq:phi_bvp}
\end{equation}

Items (a)--(f) constitute the \emph{minimal algebraic
$\phi$-closure model} used in the present exploratory fits.
Item~(g) is the next computational step (OP-new-5/OP21).


%%% -----------------------------------------------------------
\subsection{Status of the galactic closure model}
\label{subsec:lof_relation}

Galactic phenomenology in ECT is formulated in terms of the
$\phi$-sector and its effective closure relation
(Section~\ref{subsec:lof_practical}).
The present algebraic closure should be viewed as an
exploratory approximation; a full boundary-value solution
for $\phi(x)$ in realistic galactic geometry is the next
computational step (OP-new-5).


%%% -----------------------------------------------------------
\subsection{Proposed test: $g_\dagger$ variation across SPARC
galaxies}
\label{subsec:lof_gdagger_test}

A distinctive prediction of the Lorentz-order-field framework
is that $g_\dagger$ should vary weakly but systematically with
the galactic environment
(Section~\ref{subsec:lof_environment}).  Unlike standard MOND,
where $a_0$ is a fundamental constant, ECT predicts
$g_\dagger = g_\dagger(\phi_{\rm gal})$, with $\phi_{\rm gal}$
set by the ambient density.

This prediction can be tested directly on the SPARC sample as
follows:

\paragraph{Step~1: extract $g_\dagger$ per galaxy.}
For each SPARC galaxy, the effective $g_\dagger$ is obtained
directly from the $\phi$-closure fit: the best-fit
$\phi_{\rm env}$ determines
$g_\dagger = g_{\dagger 0}\,e^{\gamma\phi_{\rm env}}$.
Equivalently, one fits the BTFR form
$v_{\rm flat}^4 = G M_{\rm bar}\,g_\dagger^{(i)}$ directly,
extracting $g_\dagger^{(i)}$ as a per-galaxy quantity.

\paragraph{Step~2: assign environmental density.}
Using large-scale-structure catalogues (e.g.\ 2MRS, SDSS),
assign to each SPARC galaxy a local density
$\rho_{\rm env}^{(i)}$ (or equivalently a tidal-field
estimate, or a distance to the nearest cluster).

\paragraph{Step~3: test for correlation.}
Plot $g_\dagger^{(i)}$ versus $\rho_{\rm env}^{(i)}$ and test
for a positive correlation.  ECT predicts:
\begin{equation}
g_\dagger^{(i)} = g_0\bigl(1 + \gamma\,\delta\phi_{\rm env}^{(i)}\bigr)\,,
\qquad
\delta\phi_{\rm env}^{(i)} \propto \delta\rho_{\rm env}^{(i)}\,.
\end{equation}

\paragraph{Expected signal.}
The magnitude of the predicted modulation depends on the
parameter $\gamma$ and on the density contrast
$\delta\rho/\bar\rho$ between the galactic environment and
the cosmic mean.  As an illustrative estimate, for
$\gamma \sim 1$ and
$\delta\rho/\bar\rho \sim 1$--$10$ (cluster vs.\ void),
the modulation could be
$\delta g_\dagger/g_\dagger \sim 10\%$ or larger;
however, this number should be treated as an order-of-magnitude
indication, not a firm prediction, because $\gamma$ is not yet
derived from first principles.
The observed RAR intrinsic scatter of $0.13\,$dex ($\approx 35\%$)
is consistent with a signal of this order but also includes
observational systematics.
A positive correlation at the $\gtrsim\!2\sigma$ level would
constitute evidence for the Lorentz-order-field mechanism;
absence of correlation at $\lesssim\!5\%$ would place a
meaningful constraint on $\gamma$.

\paragraph{Distinction from MOND and $\Lambda$CDM.}
In MOND, $a_0$ is universal by construction and no
environmental correlation is expected.
In $\Lambda$CDM, there is no characteristic acceleration scale
at all; any apparent $g_\dagger$ extracted from rotation curves
should correlate with halo concentration, not with
large-scale density.
The ECT prediction is therefore qualitatively distinct from
both alternatives.


%%% -----------------------------------------------------------
\subsection{Open problems}
\label{subsec:lof_open}

\begin{description}[leftmargin=2em]
\item[OP-new-1.]
  \textbf{Non-perturbative derivation of $Y_\phi^{3/2}$.}
  Show via functional RG that the $\phi$-sector near
  $\chi \to 0$ flows to a 3D fixed-point action with the
  scale-invariant kinetic term.

\item[OP-new-2.]
  \textbf{Calculation of $g_\dagger$ from condensate parameters
  (structural scale identified; coefficient undetermined).}
  Dimensional reasoning gives $g_\dagger \propto cH_0$,
  with $cH_0 \approx 7\times10^{-10}\,$m\,s$^{-2}$
  being the natural acceleration scale of a near-critical
  cosmological vacuum.
  The observed value $g_\dagger^{\rm obs} \approx
  1.2\times10^{-10}\,$m\,s$^{-2}$ implies an
  $\mathcal{O}(1)$ coefficient $\sim\!1/6$, which cannot
  yet be derived from first principles.
  Its determination requires non-perturbative knowledge of
  $V(\phi)$ and $K(\phi)$ near $\phi = 0$.

\item[OP-new-3.]
  \textbf{Tensor-mode speed in the nonlinear regime
  (resolved at the effective scalar--tensor level).}
  Within the covariant scalar--tensor sector of the effective
  action~\eqref{eq:main_effective_action}, the coupling to the
  Ricci scalar has the form $f(\phi)R/2$ with $f$ depending
  only on $\phi$, not on its kinetic term
  $X = (\partial\phi)^2/2$.  In the standard classification
  of covariant scalar--tensor theories, this means that the
  tensor principal part of the gravitational field equations
  remains Einstein-like, so that
  $c_T = c$ identically at this level.
  The nonlinear critical-IR form
  $\mathcal{F} \propto Y_\phi^{3/2}$ modifies only the
  scalar self-interaction sector and does not enter the tensor
  propagator.
  \emph{Caveat:} the full ECT action also contains an
  anisotropic curvature term
  $\alpha_{\rm ECT}\,n_\mu n_\lambda R^{\lambda}{}_\nu$
  (Section~\ref{sec:gravity}).  Whether this term modifies
  the tensor principal symbol remains a separate open
  sub-problem.
  At the level of the scalar--tensor sector alone,
  compatibility with GW170817
  ($|c_{\rm GW}/c - 1| < 10^{-15}$) is guaranteed.

\item[OP-new-4.]
  \textbf{Cosmological evolution of $\phi$.}
  Solve the coupled $\phi$--Friedmann system to obtain
  $g_\dagger(z)$.

\item[OP-new-5.]
  \textbf{Rotation-curve refits.}
  Replace the algebraic closure with direct numerical solutions
  of~\eqref{eq:aqual_phi_main} and compare $\chi^2/N$ values.
\end{description}



%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%
\section{Rotation Curves, Scaling Relations, and Observational Tests}
\label{sec:galactic}

This section presents the observational consequences of the
Lorentz-order-field framework developed in
Section~\ref{sec:lorentz_order_field}.  The key results ---
flat rotation curves, the baryonic Tully--Fisher relation (BTFR),
the radial acceleration relation (RAR), and gravitational
lensing phenomenology --- follow from the $\phi$-AQUAL
equation~\eqref{eq:aqual_phi_main} for the field
$\phi = \ln(\chi/\chi_{\rm vac})$.

\paragraph{The $\phi$-closure model.}
For rotation-curve fitting we use the minimal algebraic
$\phi$-closure model derived in
Section~\ref{subsec:lof_practical}:
the observed acceleration $g_{\rm obs}$ satisfies
eq.~\eqref{eq:practical_closure}, with the
environment-dependent threshold
$g_\dagger(\phi_{\rm env})$ from
eq.~\eqref{eq:gdagger_practical}.
The interpolating function
$\mu(x) = x/\sqrt{1+x^2}$ follows from
$\mathcal{F}(Y)$ (eq.~\eqref{eq:F_closure}).
The rotation velocity is $v(r) = \sqrt{g_{\rm obs}\cdot r}$.
Each galaxy has one free parameter: $\phi_{\rm env}$
(the ambient Lorentz-order environment).

\paragraph{Key difference from MOND.}
In standard MOND, $a_0$ is a universal constant and the
interpolating function applies identically to all galaxies.
In ECT, $g_\dagger$ depends on the environment through
$\phi_{\rm env}$: denser environments raise $g_\dagger$
(more screening, more Newtonian), while voids lower it
(deeper into the critical branch).  MOND is recovered as
the special case $\phi_{\rm env} = 0$ (cosmic mean) for
all galaxies.

All main-text figures are computed
from the $\phi$-closure relation
eq.~\eqref{eq:practical_closure}.


\subsection{Galactic rotation curves}

The Lorentz-order field modifies the effective gravitational
coupling through the $\phi$-AQUAL equation~\eqref{eq:practical_closure}.
In dilute galactic outskirts,
where the vacuum approaches the
Euclidean$\leftrightarrow$Lorentzian critical boundary, the
nonlinear branch of the $\phi$-dynamics activates and produces
an enhancement of gravity that flattens rotation curves without
particle dark matter.

The $\phi$-AQUAL fits to five SPARC galaxies and the Milky Way
are shown in Figs.~\ref{fig:sparc_ect} and~\ref{fig:MW}.
The fitted $\phi_{\rm env}$ values and $\chi^2/N$ are collected
in Table~\ref{tab:phi_fits}.

\paragraph{Status and caveats.}
The following fits should be interpreted as exploratory
$\phi$-fits rather than as final solutions of the
Lorentz-order-field equations.  The present implementation uses an algebraic
closure relation with the environment-dependent critical
acceleration scale $g_\dagger(\phi_{\rm env})$, rather than
a full boundary-value solution for the field $\phi(x)$.  The baryonic profiles are
simplified (Freeman disc + Hernquist bulge; SPARC gas
components not included separately).  These fits are
therefore proof-of-principle tests of the $\phi$-framework,
not yet SPARC-level precision calculations.



The comparison with MOND should also be interpreted with
care: MOND corresponds to the special case
$\phi_{\rm env} = 0$, whereas the ECT closure model admits
one galaxy-dependent environment parameter.  The key
physical question is not simply whether one fit is
numerically better, but whether the inferred $\phi_{\rm env}$
values correlate with independently measured environment.

\begin{table}[htb]
\centering
\caption{Exploratory $\phi$-closure fit results.  Each galaxy is
fitted with one free parameter $\phi_{\rm env}$ (the ambient
Lorentz-order environment).  These are algebraic closure fits,
not full field solutions (see text for caveats).
$g_\dagger/a_0$ shows how the effective acceleration threshold
departs from the universal MOND value.}
\label{tab:phi_fits}
\renewcommand{\arraystretch}{1.2}
\begin{tabular}{llcccc}
\hline\hline
Galaxy & Environment & $\phi_{\rm env}$ & $g_\dagger/a_0$
  & $\chi^2\!/N$ (ECT, 1p) & $\chi^2\!/N$ (MOND, 1p) \\
\hline
NGC~6503  & void       & $-2.44$ & 0.48 & 1.8 & 4.4 \\
NGC~3198  & field      & $-0.77$ & 0.79 & 11.5 & 12.7 \\
UGC~2885  & field      & $+0.59$ & 1.19 & 8.9 & 7.9 \\
Milky Way & Local Group& $+2.15$ & 1.9  & 3.9 & 8.1 \\
NGC~2403  & M81 group  & $+4.64$ & 2.5  & 2.6 & 0.7 \\
DDO~154   & M81 group  & $+5.00$ & 2.5  & 2.8 & 2.5 \\
\hline\hline
\end{tabular}
\end{table}

\emph{Observations:}
With one free parameter each (ECT: $\phi_{\rm env}$;
MOND: $\Upsilon_\star$), the two models perform comparably
overall.  ECT is better for NGC~6503 (void galaxy) and
the Milky Way; MOND is better for NGC~2403 and DDO~154
(where the ECT $\phi_{\rm env}$ saturates at the fit
boundary).  The fitted $\phi_{\rm env}$ values show a
physically sensible pattern:
NGC~6503 (void) has the most negative $\phi_{\rm env}$
(lowest $g_\dagger$, deepest into the critical branch),
while NGC~2403 and DDO~154 (M81 group) have the most positive
(highest $g_\dagger$, more screened).
The Milky Way result is promising but should be re-tested
with a more realistic baryonic decomposition.

\emph{Saturation warning:}
For NGC~2403 and DDO~154 the fitted $\phi_{\rm env}$ saturates
at the upper boundary of the scan interval ($+3.0$).
This indicates that the current one-parameter closure model
is not yet flexible enough to fully capture the data for
these systems, and should be replaced in the next stage by
a boundary-value field solver.

\subsubsection{Limitations and methodological caveats}
\label{sec:phi_caveats}

The present $\phi$-fits are exploratory algebraic closure
calculations.  They do not yet solve the full boundary-value
problem for the Lorentz-order field $\phi(x)$, and they
employ simplified baryonic geometry (Freeman disc, Hernquist
bulge, and where available a gas component) together with a
local algebraic closure relation between $g_{\rm obs}$ and
$g_N$.  They should therefore be interpreted as
proof-of-principle tests rather than final self-consistent
$\phi$-field solutions.

The comparison with MOND and $\Lambda$CDM should also be
interpreted with care.  MOND (with free $\Upsilon_\star$)
has one free parameter per galaxy; the ECT closure model
likewise has one ($\phi_{\rm env}$).  The $\Lambda$CDM/NFW
curves have two free halo parameters ($\rho_0$,~$r_s$),
giving them greater flexibility.



\subsubsection{External-field effect in $\phi$-language}
\label{sec:efe_phi}

In the $\phi$-framework, an ambient gravitational field
raises $\phi_{\rm env}$, which
shifts $g_\dagger(\phi_{\rm env})$ upward
(eq.~\eqref{eq:gdagger_practical}).
This makes the galaxy more Newtonian (more screened), reducing
the discrepancy between $g_{\rm obs}$ and $g_N$ in the
outskirts.  The effect is illustrated in Fig.~\ref{fig:EFE},
where the same galaxy is shown for several values of
$\phi_{\rm env}$.

\subsubsection{Baryonic models and fitting procedure}

The baryonic mass distribution is modelled using an
exponential disc (Freeman~1970) with Bessel-function
rotation curve, plus a Hernquist (1990) bulge where
applicable.  The gaseous component is scaled by a factor
of 1.33 to account for helium.

For each galaxy the $\phi$-closure model has
\emph{one free parameter}: $\phi_{\rm env}$.
The baryonic profile is fixed from SPARC photometry.
MOND fits additionally allow $\Upsilon_\star$ to vary (1~parameter).
$\Lambda$CDM/NFW fits have two free halo parameters
($\log\rho_0$,~$\log r_s$).

\begin{figure}[H]
\centering
\includegraphics[width=\textwidth]{figures/fig1_SPARC_ECT_bw.png}
\caption{Rotation curves of five representative SPARC
galaxies.  Each panel shows four models compared with
SPARC data (filled symbols with error bars):
\textbf{dotted light grey}~--- baryons only (Newtonian);
\textbf{dashed grey}~--- MOND with free $\Upsilon_\star$ (1~parameter);
\textbf{dot-dashed grey}~--- $\Lambda$CDM + NFW halo
(2~parameters: $\rho_0$,~$r_s$);
\textbf{solid black}~--- ECT minimal algebraic $\phi$-closure
(1~parameter: $\phi_{\rm env}$).
The $\chi^2/N$ values for each model are shown in the
legend of every panel.
Panel~(f) summarises the $\chi^2/N$ comparison across all
five galaxies.  These are exploratory closure fits, not yet
full boundary-value solutions of the $\phi$-field equation.}
\label{fig:sparc_ect}
\end{figure}

\begin{figure}[H]
\centering

$\phi$-closure model.
Data: Sofue~(2012) for $r < 5$\,kpc (grey squares) and
Eilers~et~al.~(2019) for $5$--$26$\,kpc (dark circles).
Models:
\textbf{dotted}~--- baryons only;
\textbf{dashed}~--- MOND (1p, free $\Upsilon_\star$);

(1p, $\phi_{\rm env}$).
All $\chi^2/N$ values shown in legend.}
\label{fig:MW}
\end{figure}

\begin{figure}[H]

\includegraphics[width=\textwidth]{figures/fig2_EFE_bw.png}
\caption{Environment dependence of ECT rotation curves
in the $\phi$-framework.
Panels~(a,b): NGC~3198 and DDO~154 shown with several
values of $\phi_{\rm env}$ (void, cosmic mean, group),
illustrating how the ambient Lorentz-order background
controls the onset of the nonlinear branch.
Panel~(c): the critical acceleration scale
$g_\dagger(\phi_{\rm env}) = g_{\dagger 0}\,
e^{\gamma\phi_{\rm env}}$ with fitted galaxies marked.
Panel~(d): correlation of fitted $\phi_{\rm env}$ with

\label{fig:EFE}

\subsection{Baryonic Tully--Fisher Relation}

A key empirical regularity closely related to the RAR is the baryonic
Tully--Fisher relation (BTFR), which links the asymptotic (flat) rotation
speed of a galaxy to its total baryonic mass,
\begin{equation}
v_{\rm flat}^4 \propto M_{\rm bar}.
\end{equation}
Within ECT this scaling arises naturally once the large-radius behaviour
of the effective coupling and the emergent acceleration scale $g_\dagger$
are taken into account.

\paragraph{BTFR from the $\phi$-closure.}
In the deep-critical regime of the Lorentz-order-field closure
(Section~\ref{subsec:lof_btfr}),
$\mu_n(x) \to x$, so the closure relation
$\mu_n(g_{\rm obs}/g_\dagger)\,g_{\rm obs} = g_N$ becomes
\begin{equation}
\frac{g_{\rm obs}^2}{g_\dagger(\phi_{\rm env})} \approx g_N\,.
\end{equation}
For an outer circular orbit where $g_N = G M_{\rm bar}/r^2$
and the enclosed baryonic mass has saturated, writing
$v_c^2 = r\,g_{\rm obs}$ gives
\begin{equation}
g_{\rm obs} \approx \sqrt{g_N\,g_\dagger(\phi_{\rm env})}
\label{eq:vflat_r0}
\end{equation}
and therefore
\begin{equation}
v_c^4 \approx G\,M_{\rm bar}\,g_\dagger(\phi_{\rm env})\,,
\label{eq:BTFR_from_ECT}
\end{equation}
which is precisely the baryonic Tully--Fisher scaling.
The environment-dependent scale
$g_\dagger(\phi_{\rm env}) = g_{\dagger 0}\,e^{\gamma\phi_{\rm env}}$
enters as the only non-baryonic parameter.

\paragraph{Interpretation.}
Equation~\eqref{eq:BTFR_from_ECT} shows that once a near-universal
emergent scale $g_\dagger$ is established by condensate physics, the
BTFR follows with no additional free parameters. Conversely, departures
from strict universality of $g_\dagger$ (e.g.\ due to environment-dependent
suppression effects) translate into controlled, testable deviations from
the ideal BTFR slope.






\subsection{Radial Acceleration Relation}

One of the most striking empirical regularities in galactic dynamics is
the radial acceleration relation (RAR), which connects the observed
gravitational acceleration in galaxies to that predicted from their
baryonic mass distribution.

\paragraph{ECT description.}
In the revised ECT framework the RAR is described primarily through
the Lorentz-order-field closure relation: given the Newtonian
baryonic acceleration
\begin{equation}
g_{\mathrm{bar}}(r)=\frac{G_N\,M_{\mathrm{bar}}(r)}{r^2}\,,
\end{equation}
the observed acceleration satisfies
\begin{equation}
\mu_n\!\left(\frac{g_{\rm obs}}{g_\dagger(\phi_{\rm env})}\right)
g_{\rm obs} = g_{\rm bar}\,.
\end{equation}
This equation is the theoretical prediction; the empirical RAR
serves as the observational comparator.

\paragraph{Empirical comparator.}
For comparison with the observational literature, the empirical
RAR can be written phenomenologically as
\begin{equation}
\label{eq:RAR_empirical}
g_{\mathrm{obs}}
=
\frac{g_{\mathrm{bar}}}
{1-\exp\!\left(-\sqrt{g_{\mathrm{bar}}/g_\dagger}\right)} ,
\end{equation}
with the empirically inferred acceleration scale
\begin{equation}
\label{eq:gdagger_empirical}
g_\dagger \approx 1.2\times10^{-10}\ \mathrm{m\,s^{-2}} .
\end{equation}
This empirical curve is not taken here as a theoretical input
but as a data summary against which the $\phi$-closure
predictions are tested.

In the $\Lambda$CDM framework the RAR emerges from a complex
interplay between baryonic and dark matter distributions.

\paragraph{Acceleration scale in ECT.}

In ECT the transition between the Newtonian regime and the modified
galactic regime is controlled by the critical scale $g_\dagger(\phi_{\rm env})$.
In the revised framework the critical scale is treated directly
as an environment-controlled quantity,
\begin{equation}
\label{eq:gdagger_def}
g_\dagger(\phi_{\rm env})
= g_{\dagger 0}\,e^{\gamma\phi_{\rm env}}\,,
\end{equation}
whose detailed derivation from the microscopic condensate dynamics
remains open.  At the present stage it enters phenomenology through
the exploratory closure relation.

In the $\phi$-framework the BTFR normalization is controlled
by $g_\dagger(\phi_{\rm env})$, which depends on the ambient
Lorentz-order background.
Numerically, for representative late-type galaxies one finds
\begin{equation}
g_\dagger \sim 10^{-10}\,\mathrm{m\,s^{-2}},
\end{equation}
consistent with the empirical scale \eqref{eq:gdagger_empirical}.

\paragraph{ECT interpretation of the RAR.}

The $\phi$-closure (eq.~\eqref{eq:practical_closure})
directly determines the observed acceleration from
$g_{\rm bar}(r)$ and $g_\dagger(\phi_{\rm env})$,
with one environment parameter $\phi_{\rm env}$ per galaxy.
The theory is therefore formulated in terms of $g_{\rm obs}$
rather than an effective running $G_{\rm eff}(r)$.

\paragraph{Limiting regimes.}

For strong gravitational fields $g_{\mathrm{bar}}\gg g_\dagger$, one
recovers the Newtonian limit,
\begin{equation}
g_{\mathrm{obs}}\approx g_{\mathrm{bar}}.
\end{equation}
In the opposite regime, $g_{\mathrm{bar}} \ll g_\dagger$, one obtains
\begin{equation}
g_{\mathrm{obs}} \approx \sqrt{g_{\mathrm{bar}}\,g_\dagger},
\end{equation}
which coincides with the deep-MOND limit.

\paragraph{Remark: cosmological coincidence.}
Interestingly, the observed value of $g_\dagger$ is numerically close to
the cosmological scale $cH_0$ (and equivalently $\sim c^2\sqrt{\Lambda}$),
a relation that has been noted in the MOND literature.
Within ECT this suggests that the galactic critical scale
$g_\dagger(\phi_{\rm env})$ may be indirectly linked to the late-time
cosmological state of the Lorentz-order background.
Establishing this link from first principles remains a task for
future work.

\paragraph{Status of the $g_\dagger$ scale.}
In the present formulation, the emergence of a near-universal $g_\dagger$
is supported by the $\phi$-closure
(eq.~\eqref{eq:gdagger_def}), in which $g_\dagger$ depends on
the ambient Lorentz-order background $\phi_{\rm env}$.
A first-principles derivation of
$g_\dagger(\phi_{\rm env},\mathrm{environment})$
from the microscopic condensate dynamics is an open problem and
requires dedicated numerical modelling. Throughout this work we therefore
treat the universality of $g_\dagger$ as a derived \emph{effective}
consequence of condensate phenomenology, and we use it to generate
falsifiable predictions (e.g.\ BTFR and EFE trends) rather than as an
assumed constant of nature.

\paragraph{Predictive aspect.}

Within the revised ECT framework, the key predictive question is
whether the fitted environment parameter $\phi_{\rm env}$ correlates
with independently inferred environment measures and whether a single
$\phi$-closure can simultaneously account for rotation curves, BTFR,
and RAR.  This is illustrated in Fig.~\ref{fig:rar}.

\paragraph{Universal ECT interpolation function.}

The per-galaxy ECT prediction
$g_{\rm obs} = \mathcal{G}(g_{\rm bar};\,g_\dagger(\phi_{\rm env}))$ (see eq.~\eqref{eq:practical_closure})
can be cast as a universal interpolation function on the
$(g_{\rm bar},\,g_{\rm obs})$ plane.
In the $\phi$-closure framework the per-galaxy RAR follows
directly from the closure relation
(eq.~\eqref{eq:practical_closure}):
\begin{equation}
\label{eq:rar_ect_universal}
g_{\rm obs}
= \mathcal{G}(g_{\rm bar};\,g_\dagger(\phi_{\rm env}),\,n)\,,
\end{equation}
where $g_\dagger(\phi_{\rm env})$ plays the role of the
galaxy-specific acceleration scale.  For the well-constrained
spirals the fitted $g_\dagger$ values are within a factor of
$\sim\!2$ of $a_0 = 1.2\times10^{-10}\,$m\,s$^{-2}$,
confirming that the $\phi$-framework reproduces the empirical
MOND acceleration scale.

\paragraph{Comparison of interpolation functions.}

The ECT interpolation~\eqref{eq:rar_ect_universal} and the empirical
RAR~\eqref{eq:RAR_empirical} share the same asymptotic behavior:
$g_{\rm obs} \to g_{\rm bar}$ for $g_{\rm bar} \gg g^\dagger$
(Newtonian) and
$g_{\rm obs} \to \sqrt{g^\dagger\,g_{\rm bar}}$ for
$g_{\rm bar} \ll g^\dagger$ (deep-MOND).
They are however structurally distinct in the transition
region near~$g^\dagger$: the maximum relative deviation is
$\sim$8\% at $g_{\rm bar} \approx 0.4\,g^\dagger$, well within
the observational scatter of the RAR data.
Both functions belong to the general class of interpolating
functions with the correct Newtonian and deep-MOND asymptotes
\cite{Milgrom1983,Famaey2012}, but the ECT function is
\emph{derived} from the condensate dynamics rather than
postulated phenomenologically.

\paragraph{Honest assessment.}

A quantitative comparison with the SPARC database
\cite{Lelli2016} is summarised in Table~\ref{tab:phi_fits}.
At equal parameter count (one per galaxy), ECT and MOND
perform comparably overall:
ECT ($\chi^2/N \sim 1.8$--$11.5$, one parameter $\phi_{\rm env}$)
is favoured for some void-like and Local-Group systems,
while MOND ($\chi^2/N \sim 0.7$--$12.7$, one parameter
$\Upsilon_\star$) is favoured for some group-environment
galaxies.
$\Lambda$CDM/NFW ($\chi^2/N \sim 0.2$--$8.1$, two halo
parameters) has greater flexibility and fits best in most cases.
The present algebraic $\phi$-closure does not yet capture
the full inner morphology of all rotation curves;
a more refined $\phi$-field solution may improve the fits.
The computational details of the SPARC analysis are given in
the code repository (see Data and Code Availability).


\begin{figure*}[!htb]
\centering
\includegraphics[width=\textwidth]{figures/gif6_ECT_RAR_6panel.png}
\caption{Radial Acceleration Relation (RAR) from the SPARC database
and the ECT $\phi$-closure prediction.
In all panels the thin diagonal line marks
$g_{\rm obs}=g_{\rm bar}$ (Newtonian, no dark matter) and the
deep-MOND asymptote $g_{\rm obs}=\sqrt{g_\dagger\,g_{\rm bar}}$
is shown as a thin dashed line.  Both reference lines are labelled.
%
\textbf{Panels~(a)--(e):} individual galaxies.
Data points with error bars are from SPARC.
\textbf{Dashed grey}: MOND with universal $a_0$
(using the galaxy's baryonic profile).
\textbf{Solid black}: ECT $\phi$-closure model with fitted
$\phi_{\rm env}$ (one parameter per galaxy).
The thin solid curve shows the empirical RAR of McGaugh~et~al.~(2016).
%
\textbf{Panel~(f):} combined view.
Gray dots: simulated SPARC-like scatter from 175 galaxies.
Distinct markers: the five galaxies from panels~(a)--(e).
The thick solid curve is the RAR; the dashed and dot-dashed
curves show the ECT $\phi$-family for void ($\phi_{\rm env}=-2$)
and group ($\phi_{\rm env}=+3$) environments.
The shaded region between the Newtonian line and the RAR represents
the ``missing gravity''.
These are exploratory closure fits, not yet full $\phi$-field solutions.}
\label{fig:rar}
\end{figure*}


%%% -----------------------------------------------------------
\subsection{Per-galaxy acceleration scale: a test of
$\phi$-logic vs.\ universal $a_0$}
\label{subsec:gdagger_per_galaxy}

A central prediction of the Lorentz-order-field framework
is that the effective acceleration scale
$g_\dagger(\phi_{\rm env})$ should \emph{not} be exactly
universal, but should depend weakly on the ambient
Lorentz-order background.

For the five explicitly fitted SPARC galaxies
(Section~\ref{sec:galactic}), the per-galaxy values
are:

\begin{center}
\begin{tabular}{llccc}
\hline\hline
Galaxy & Type & $\phi_{\rm env}$ & $g_\dagger(\phi_{\rm env})$ (m\,s$^{-2}$) & $g_\dagger/a_0$ \\
\hline
UGC~2885  & Massive spiral & $+0.6$ & $1.4\times10^{-10}$ & 1.2 \\
NGC~3198  & Sb spiral      & $-0.8$ & $9.5\times10^{-11}$ & 0.79 \\
NGC~6503  & Sc spiral      & $-2.4$ & $5.8\times10^{-11}$ & 0.48 \\
NGC~2403  & Scd spiral     & $+4.6$ & $3.0\times10^{-10}$ & 2.5 \\
DDO~154   & Dwarf irregular& $+5.0$ & $3.0\times10^{-10}$ & 2.5 \\
\hline\hline
\end{tabular}
\end{center}

\noindent
\emph{Observations:}
\begin{enumerate}
\item The three luminous spirals (NGC~3198, NGC~6503,
  UGC~2885) have $g_\dagger^{\rm eff}$ within an order of
  magnitude --- quasi-universal, as expected when
  $\phi_{\rm gal}$ is dominated by the cosmological background.

\item The dwarf DDO~154 shows $g_\dagger^{\rm eff}$
  roughly two orders of magnitude higher.  In MOND this would
  be anomalous.  In the $\phi$-framework it is
  \emph{expected}: low-mass systems reside in shallower
  potential wells, have lower ambient $\phi_{\rm gal}$,
  and therefore reach the critical branch at a different
  effective threshold.

\item The scatter across the five galaxies
  ($\sim\!0.6\,$dex) exceeds the RAR intrinsic scatter
  ($0.13\,$dex).  Part of this excess likely reflects
  limitations of the current closure model: DDO~154
  in particular saturates the $\phi_{\rm env}$ fit boundary.
  However, any \emph{systematic} residual trend with mass
  or environment would be a direct signature of the
  $\phi$-mechanism.
\end{enumerate}

\paragraph{Testable distinction from MOND.}
In standard MOND, $a_0$ is a universal constant; any
per-galaxy scatter is observational noise.
In ECT, $g_\dagger$ inherits a physical dependence on
$\phi_{\rm gal}$ and should therefore correlate with
the ambient density environment.  The prediction is:
\emph{at fixed baryonic mass, cluster galaxies should
show systematically higher $g_\dagger$ than void dwarfs.}
This test can be performed on the full 148-galaxy SPARC
sample cross-matched with large-scale-structure catalogues
(Section~\ref{subsec:lof_gdagger_test}).

\begin{figure}[htb]
\centering
\includegraphics[width=\textwidth]{figures/fig_gdagger_analysis.png}
\caption{Per-galaxy effective acceleration scale
$g_\dagger(\phi_{\rm env})$ from ECT $\phi$-closure fits.  \textbf{(a)}~$g_\dagger^{\rm eff}$ versus stellar mass;
the shaded band shows the MOND intrinsic scatter ($0.13\,$dex)
around $a_0$.  \textbf{(b)}~Ratio $g_\dagger^{\rm eff}/a_0$;
MOND predicts all bars at unity.  The large scatter,
especially for the dwarf DDO~154, is consistent with the
$\phi$-framework prediction that $g_\dagger$ is
environment-dependent.}
\label{fig:gdagger_analysis}
\end{figure}

\paragraph{Reading the figure.}

Panel~(a) plots the effective critical acceleration
$g_\dagger^{\rm eff}$ extracted from each $\phi$-closure
fit against the galaxy's stellar mass.
The dashed line marks the MOND value $a_0 = 1.2\times10^{-10}\,$m/s$^2$
and the shaded band its observed intrinsic scatter (0.13\,dex).
The data points scatter over $\sim\!0.6\,$dex ---
far more than MOND allows.
In MOND, every point must sit inside the grey band;
in ECT, the scatter is physical and reflects different
$\phi_{\rm env}$.

Panel~(b) shows the ratio $g_\dagger^{\rm eff}/a_0$ for
each galaxy, with horizontal bars indicating the density
of its environment (labels: Local Group, field, void,
M81 group).
MOND predicts all bars at unity (vertical dashed line).
ECT predicts a spread correlated with environment:
galaxies in denser environments (groups) should have
\emph{higher} $g_\dagger$, while isolated galaxies
(voids) should have \emph{lower} $g_\dagger$.
The data show exactly this pattern:
NGC~2403 and DDO~154 (M81 group) have $g_\dagger/a_0 \approx 2.5$,
while NGC~6503 (void) has $g_\dagger/a_0 \approx 0.48$.
This environment--$g_\dagger$ correlation is the most
distinctive falsifiable prediction of ECT (prediction F1
in Table~\ref{tab:ECT_predictions}).

\paragraph{Relation to MOND failures.}
Several known anomalies of MOND --- including the Bullet
Cluster mass-discrepancy, the too-small EFE in some
field dwarfs, and the cluster-mass discrepancy ---
can be reinterpreted in the ECT framework as signatures
of environment-dependent $g_\dagger$ rather than
fundamental failures of the acceleration-based approach.
MOND, in this view, is an excellent approximation to
the critical branch of ECT, but its insistence on a
\emph{universal} $a_0$ causes it to fail precisely where
the ambient Lorentz-order background departs most from
the cosmological mean.


%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%

\subsection{Gravitational lensing}

In the revised $\phi$-framework, weak-field lensing should
ultimately be computed from the metric potentials induced by
the Lorentz-order field, with possible gravitational slip
($\Phi \neq \Psi$) in dilute environments where the
$\phi$-branch is active.

The Lorentz-order field contributes an anomalous deflection
controlled by gradients of $\phi$:
\begin{equation}
\delta\varphi_{\rm ECT} \;\propto\;
\frac{1}{c^2}
\int \nabla_\perp \phi\, dl\,,
\label{eq:anomalous_lensing}
\end{equation}
where $\nabla_\perp$ is the gradient transverse to the line
of sight.  This term produces lensing that traces the
Lorentz-order-field distribution
rather than the instantaneous matter distribution.

\paragraph{Gravitational slip in dilute environments.}
Because the Lorentz-order field modifies the effective metric
rather than merely the non-relativistic force law, the ratio
between the dynamical potential felt by nonrelativistic matter
and the lensing potential governing null geodesics need not
remain fixed at the GR value in dilute near-critical
environments.  A systematic derivation of the two metric
potentials (Bardeen $\Phi$ and $\Psi$) in the $\phi$-branch
is a priority for future work (OP21); if confirmed, this
gravitational slip would be an additional testable signature
distinct from MOND, where the lensing-to-dynamics ratio is
universal by construction.

Because the $\phi$-field can develop spatial gradients during
dynamical processes (such as galaxy or cluster mergers), the effective
gravitational potential does not necessarily trace the instantaneous gas
distribution. In particular, the Lorentz-order background may remain
associated with collisionless galaxy components while the gas component
experiences ram-pressure stripping.

\begin{figure}[H]
\centering
\includegraphics[width=0.75\textwidth]{figures/fig_bullet_cluster.png}
\caption{Schematic ECT interpretation of the Bullet Cluster
(1E~0657$-$558).  Solid: galaxy (stellar) distribution showing
main cluster and infalling subcluster.
Dashed: X-ray gas, displaced from the galaxies by ram-pressure
stripping during the collision.
Dotted: ECT lensing convergence, controlled by the
Lorentz-order field $\phi$
(Section~\ref{sec:lorentz_order_field}).
Because $\phi$ is enhanced by matter density
($d\bar\phi/d\rho > 0$), the ECT lensing peaks coincide with
the collisionless galaxy positions---reproducing the observed
offset between lensing and X-ray peaks without invoking
particle dark matter \cite{Clowe2006,Markevitch2004}.}
\label{fig:bullet_cluster}
\end{figure}

\paragraph{Reading the figure.}

Figure~\ref{fig:bullet_cluster} shows the three components
after the cluster collision.
\emph{Solid ellipses}: the two stellar (galaxy) distributions,
which passed through each other ballistically.
\emph{Dashed contour} (centre): X-ray gas, decelerated and
stripped by ram pressure, containing $\sim\!5\times$ more
baryonic mass than the stars.
\emph{Dotted contours with crosshairs}: lensing convergence
peaks, controlled by the $\phi$-field.

In $\Lambda$CDM, the lensing peaks track invisible DM halos
that passed through with the stars.
In ECT, the same offset arises because $\phi$ responds to
\emph{local density}: compact stellar clumps create deeper
$\phi$-wells than the diffuse gas of the same total mass.
No particle dark matter is invoked.





%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%

\subsection{Bullet Cluster}

The Bullet Cluster (1E 0657–558) represents one of the most frequently
discussed observational challenges in theories that attempt to modify
gravity without introducing dark matter \cite{Clowe2006,Markevitch2004}.

Observations show that during the collision of two galaxy clusters the
hot intracluster gas — which contains the majority of baryonic mass —
is strongly decelerated by hydrodynamic interactions.  However, weak
gravitational lensing maps reveal that the gravitational potential is
spatially displaced from the gas distribution and instead closely
traces the distribution of galaxies.

Within the $\Lambda$CDM paradigm this observation is interpreted as
evidence for collisionless dark matter that passes through the
interaction region with little resistance.

\paragraph{ECT interpretation.}

In the revised $\phi$-framework the gravitational lensing potential
is controlled by the Lorentz-order field $\phi$
(Section~\ref{sec:lorentz_order_field}).  Matter density enhances
$\phi$ ($d\bar\phi/d\rho > 0$,
Section~\ref{subsec:lof_matter}), so the $\phi$-profile traces
primarily the collisionless stellar component rather than the
hydrodynamically stripped gas.

During a cluster merger, stars traverse the collision region almost
ballistically while the gas experiences strong ram-pressure drag.
The Lorentz-order field, being sourced by the local matter density,
follows the stellar component.  The lensing potential, which depends
on the metric potentials induced by $\phi$, therefore peaks at the
galaxy positions rather than at the gas positions.

\paragraph{Quantitative assessment.}

The cluster-merger phenomenology separates into two
logically distinct questions that should not be conflated.

\textit{Question 1: Offset morphology (success).}
The stellar component after collision is concentrated
within $R_\star \sim 80\,$kpc, while the ram-pressure-stripped
gas fills $R_{\rm gas} \sim 500\,$kpc.
The local density ratio is
$\rho_\star/\rho_{\rm gas} \sim 50$.
Since $\phi$ is enhanced by local density
($d\bar\phi/d\rho > 0$), the lensing convergence peaks at
the galaxy positions --- reproducing the observed offset
$\Delta\theta_{\rm obs} \approx 25''$ ($\approx 100\,$kpc
at $z = 0.296$) without invoking particle dark matter.

\textit{Question 2: Total lensing amplitude (open tension).}

The honest ECT calculation uses only the $\phi$-field equation
and the observed baryonic mass --- no additional components.
For spherical symmetry, the algebraic $\phi$-closure
$\mu(g/g_\dagger)\,g = g_{\rm bar}$ is the \emph{exact}
solution of the field equation, so the result below is not
an approximation within the spherical ansatz.

For the Bullet Cluster, the baryonic mass profile is modelled
as an isothermal $\beta$-model gas
($M_{\rm gas} \approx 1.2\times10^{14}\,M_\odot$,
core radius $r_c \approx 200\,$kpc) plus a compact stellar
component ($M_\star \approx 8\times10^{12}\,M_\odot$,
$r_c \approx 80\,$kpc), consistent with X-ray and optical observations \cite{Paraficz2016,Markevitch2004}.  The ECT effective mass
$M_{\rm ECT}(<R) = \mu_\phi(R)\,M_{\rm bar}(<R)$
is compared directly with the observed lensing mass
at the same aperture (Table~\ref{tab:bullet_results}):

\begin{table}[h]
\centering
\small
\caption{Bullet Cluster: ECT $\phi$-closure results.
Baryonic profile: isothermal $\beta$-model gas
($M_{\rm gas} \approx 1.2\times10^{14}\,M_\odot$)
plus compact stellar component
($M_\star \approx 8\times10^{12}\,M_\odot$).
For spherical symmetry the algebraic $\phi$-closure
is exact.  A complementary 2D projected solver
(Appendix~\ref{app:cluster_lensing}) gives consistent
results within the systematic uncertainties of both
approaches.
Code: \url{https://github.com/chufelo/ECT-preprint-code}.}
\label{tab:bullet_results}
\begin{tabular}{rccccc}
\toprule
$R$ (kpc) & $M_{\rm bar}(<R)$ & $g_{\rm bar}/g_\dagger$ &
  $\mu_\phi$ & $M_{\rm ECT}$ & $M_{\rm ECT}/M_{\rm obs}$ \\
\midrule
250  & $8.7\times10^{13}$ & 1.6  & 1.14 &
  $9.9\times10^{13}$  & 0.45 \\
500  & $1.2\times10^{14}$ & 0.56 & 1.53 &
  $1.9\times10^{14}$  & 0.37 \\
1000 & $1.3\times10^{14}$ & 0.15 & 2.70 &
  $3.4\times10^{14}$  & 0.34 \\
1500 & $1.3\times10^{14}$ & 0.07 & 3.96 &
  $5.1\times10^{14}$  & 0.34 \\
\bottomrule
\end{tabular}
\end{table}

These are order-of-magnitude estimates.
A factor-of-two systematic uncertainty is inherent in
the spherical $\beta$-model approximation applied to a
non-spherical merger system.
The three uncomputed effects --- environmental $g_\dagger$
modulation, gravitational slip, and non-equilibrium merger
dynamics --- can each individually shift the result at
order unity.
Therefore the present deficit of factor $\sim\!2$--$3$
is \emph{within the expected accuracy of the method}
and should not be interpreted as a definitive quantitative
failure of ECT.
A full 3D merger-scale metric--$\phi$ solver (OP22) is
required to determine whether the deficit persists or
is absorbed by these corrections.

The $\phi$-branch explains approximately
\textbf{one-third to one-half} of the observed lensing mass
across all apertures.  At small radii ($R \lesssim 250\,$kpc),
$g_{\rm bar}/g_\dagger \gtrsim 1$ and the system is in the
screened/transition regime ($\mu_\phi \approx 1.1$), so the
ECT enhancement is modest.
At large radii ($R \gtrsim 1\,$Mpc), the system enters the
deep $\phi$-branch ($\mu_\phi \approx 4$), but the deficit
factor remains $\sim\!2$--$3$.

This represents a genuine \emph{cluster mass discrepancy}
in ECT, comparable in magnitude to the well-known MOND
cluster problem (where a residual factor $\sim\!2$ is
typically required).

\paragraph{What this does and does not mean.}

The deficit is a prediction of the current ECT $\phi$-closure,
not an artefact of a particular ansatz.
It should be reported honestly rather than hidden behind
phenomenological parameters.

Three effects not included in the present estimate may
change the quantitative result, but their magnitude is
currently unknown:
\emph{(i)}~environmental modulation of $g_\dagger$ on
cluster scales (prediction F1, untested numerically);
\emph{(ii)}~gravitational slip
($\Phi_{\rm lens} \neq \Phi_{\rm dyn}$), which could
amplify lensing relative to dynamics (OP21, not derived);
\emph{(iii)}~non-equilibrium $\phi$-dynamics during the
merger, which break the quasi-static assumption used above.

Until these effects are computed from first principles,
the cluster mass discrepancy remains an open problem in ECT.

\paragraph{Qualitative success across all cluster types.}

A 2D projected $\phi$-closure solver (thin-lens approximation)
was applied to four merging clusters:
Bullet~\cite{Clowe2006,Paraficz2016},
MACS~J0025~\cite{Bradac2008},
El~Gordo~\cite{Diego2023}, and
Abell~520~\cite{Mahdavi2007}.
In all four systems the convergence peaks follow the
component with higher \emph{local} surface density:
compact stellar clumps in Bullet, MACS~J0025, and El~Gordo;
the dense central gas core in Abell~520.
This is precisely the ECT prediction: the $\phi$-field
responds to local compactness and density contrast, not to
a universal collisionless species.
The fact that ECT naturally reproduces \emph{both}
the stellar-tracking morphology (Bullet-type) \emph{and}
the gas-tracking morphology (Abell~520-type) is a strong
qualitative discriminant relative to both $\Lambda$CDM
(which always predicts collisionless DM halos at the
stellar positions) and MOND (which has no lensing mechanism
at all for offset prediction).

\paragraph{Lensing offset morphology: four-cluster test.}

A 2D projected $\phi$-closure solver
(Appendix~\ref{app:cluster_lensing}) was applied to four
merging clusters to test the offset prediction.
Table~\ref{tab:4cluster} summarises the results.

\begin{table}[h]
\centering
\small

(2D projected $\phi$-closure).
$d_{\star\to\kappa}$: distance from $\kappa$-peaks to
stellar peaks; $d_{{\rm gas}\to\kappa}$: to gas peaks.
In all four systems the $\kappa$-peaks follow the component
with higher local surface density.
Code: \url{https://github.com/chufelo/ECT-preprint-code}.}
\label{tab:4cluster}

\cite{Bradac2008} \\

\end{tabular}
\end{table}


\paragraph{Limitations of the present estimate.}

The values of $R_{\rm cl}$ in
Table~\ref{tab:4cluster} should be understood as
order-of-magnitude indicators, not precision results.
The current calculation relies on several simplifications
whose combined effect could shift the numbers by a factor
of $\sim\!2$ in either direction.

\textit{(i) Algebraic closure vs.\ field solution.}
The $\phi$-closure $\mu(g/g_\dagger)\,g = g_{\rm bar}$
is an algebraic relation derived for quasi-static,
spherically symmetric systems.
A cluster merger is neither static nor spherical.
The full calculation requires solving the nonlinear
field equation
$\nabla\!\cdot\![\mu_\phi(|\nabla\phi|)\,\nabla\phi]
= S[\rho_{\rm bar}]$
on a 2D/3D grid, which can concentrate the $\phi$-response
differently from the algebraic proxy.

\textit{(ii) Lensing $\neq$ dynamics.}
The numbers above assume that the lensing enhancement
equals the dynamical enhancement.
In a scalar-tensor theory the lensing potential
$(\Phi + \Psi)/2$ can differ from the dynamical potential
$\Phi$ by a gravitational slip factor.
Even a modest slip $\eta \equiv \Psi/\Phi \neq 1$ would
amplify the lensing signal relative to the dynamical one,
potentially closing part of the residual.

\textit{(iii) Non-stationarity and merger memory.}
The Bullet Cluster is a dynamical collision, not a
relaxed system.
The $\phi$-field may retain memory of the pre-collision
configuration, producing transient gradients that enhance
the lensing signal at the positions of the compact
collisionless clumps.
This effect is absent in any quasi-static estimate.

\textit{(iv) Aperture sensitivity.}
The baryon fraction $f_b$ depends critically on the
aperture in which it is measured.
For the Bullet Cluster, $f_b$ ranges from $\sim\!0.04$
at $R_{200}$ to $\sim\!0.22$ within 250\,kpc.
This factor-of-five variation directly propagates into
$\mathcal{E}_{\rm req}$ and $R_{\rm cl}$.
The values in Table~\ref{tab:4cluster} use
aperture-matched $f_b$ from recent JWST and strong-lensing
analyses, but these carry systematic uncertainties of
order 30--50\%.

\textit{(v) Cluster-specific physics.}
Each system has distinctive features that a single
$\mu_{\rm cl}$ cannot capture:
the Bullet Cluster has an unusually high-velocity collision
($v \sim 4700\,$km/s) that maximises the gas--stellar
separation;
Abell~520 has a dense gas core that \emph{coincides}
with the lensing peak (opposite to Bullet), which in ECT
is natural because $\phi$ follows local density regardless
of whether the dense component is stellar or gaseous;
MACS~J0025 has a very low stellar fraction, making it the
hardest case for any modified-gravity explanation;
El~Gordo at $z = 0.87$ probes a higher-redshift
$\phi$-background where $g_\dagger(z)$ may differ from
the local value (prediction~F10).

The present factor-of-few cluster tension should therefore
not be interpreted as a definitive failure of ECT.
At the same time, it is too large to be dismissed as a
minor approximation error.
A full 2D/3D merger-scale $\phi$-solver is required to
determine whether the Bullet Cluster can be saturated by
the $\phi$-branch alone or whether a residual collisionless
condensate sector is necessary (OP22, OP23).

\paragraph{Comparison with modified-gravity models.}

In purely geometric modifications of gravity the gravitational
potential is typically tied directly to the baryonic mass
distribution.  Such models therefore have difficulty explaining
the separation between gas and gravitational lensing signal.
In ECT the additional condensate degree of freedom naturally
introduces a dynamical mechanism allowing the potential to
follow the collisionless component.

\paragraph{Observational tests.}

A characteristic prediction of this scenario is that the displacement
between the lensing peak and the gas should correlate with the
fraction of collisionless matter in the system.
Future observations of cluster mergers could therefore provide a
quantitative test of the model.





\paragraph{Remark: effective dark-sector phenomenology from the condensate.}
In the Lorentz-order-field framework the above astrophysical
effects arise from the environment-dependent nonlinear response
of the vacuum order parameter $\phi$, without introducing
additional collisionless particle matter.  Whether ECT also
predicts independent dark-sector degrees of freedom (e.g.\
Goldstone modes or topological objects) is a separate question
discussed in Section~\ref{sec:dark_energy_phi}.







\section{Cosmological Implications}
\label{sec:cosmology}

The condensate framework provides a natural setting for
early-universe cosmology, dark energy, and the evolution of
fundamental constants.  This section discusses the pre-Planckian
Euclidean phase, inflation, the dark energy equation of state,
and the Hubble tension.

The Lorentz-order field introduced in
Section~\ref{sec:lorentz_order_field} should not be viewed
as a purely galactic variable.  In a homogeneous cosmological
setting, one may regard $\phi_{\rm bg}(t)$ as the
time-dependent background degree of Lorentzian ordering of
the vacuum.  The galactic acceleration scale $g_\dagger$,
the effective late-time scalar response, and possible
departures from $\Lambda$CDM may then be interpreted as
different manifestations of the same evolving order parameter.
A central next step is to unify the homogeneous cosmological
background $\phi_{\rm bg}(t)$ with the inhomogeneous galactic
order field $\phi(x)$, thereby connecting late-time cosmology,
the evolution of $g_\dagger$, lensing, redshift anomalies,
and galactic dynamics within one common vacuum-order framework.

\subsection{Early-universe phase transition}
\label{sec:cosmo:pt}

A fundamental difference between ECT and the standard $\Lambda$CDM
framework concerns the status of the pre-Planckian era.
In $\Lambda$CDM there is no meaningful physics before the Planck time
$t_{\rm Pl} \approx 5.4\times10^{-44}$\,s: spacetime itself is
undefined, and the initial singularity marks the boundary of the
theory.

In ECT the situation is qualitatively different.
The four-dimensional Euclidean manifold $\mathcal{M} = \mathbb{R}^4$
exists as a well-defined physical arena \emph{before} the phase
transition.
In this pre-temporal Euclidean phase the O(4) symmetry is unbroken,
the condensate VEV vanishes ($v_0 = 0$), and there is no Lorentzian
signature---hence no time coordinate, no causal structure, and no
arrow of time.
The field $\Phi$ fluctuates around $\langle\Phi\rangle = 0$ in a
fully O(4)-symmetric state.

The phase transition O(4)$\to$O(3) occurs when the anisotropy
parameter exceeds unity ($\alpha > 1$), generating a Lorentzian
effective metric
$g_{\mu\nu}^{\rm eff}
= \mathrm{diag}\bigl(-(\alpha-\beta),\,\beta,\,\beta,\,\beta\bigr)/\beta$
[$\alpha=2\beta$, $\beta=1$: $\mathrm{diag}(-1,1,1,1)$]
with the effective speed of light
$c_* = \sqrt{\beta/(\alpha-\beta)}$ [$\alpha=2\beta$, $\beta=1$: $c_*=1$].
This is analogous to a condensation transition in a superfluid
\cite{Volovik2003}, producing a ``before'' that is pre-temporal
rather than nonexistent.
The analogy with the Hartle--Hawking no-boundary proposal
\cite{HartleHawking1983} is notable, but in ECT the Euclidean
regime is the \emph{fundamental} state, not a mathematical
analytic continuation.

The arrow of time emerges from the large-$N_{\rm eff}$ decoherence
in the symmetry-broken phase: macroscopic systems satisfy
$\Gamma_{\rm irr} \gg 1$ within the Gaussian--Markov model,
yielding the effective second law $dS_{\rm ent}/dw \geq 0$ (Level~B, OP24).

This pre-temporal Euclidean phase has no counterpart in $\Lambda$CDM
and constitutes the most radical conceptual prediction of ECT
(see Fig.~\ref{fig:timeline}).


\subsection{Inflation as condensate formation}
\label{sec:inflation}
\label{sec:cosmo:inflation}
\label{subsec:inflation_mechanism}

\paragraph{Pre-inflationary phase.}
Before the $O(4)\to O(3)$ transition the condensate order parameter
vanishes: $\langle\partial_A\Phi\rangle = 0$.  The field is in the
symmetric phase $\Phi=0$ and the potential energy density equals
$V(0)=0$ (with the conventions of Postulate~P4).  There is no preferred
direction, no emergent metric, and no notion of time.  This
\emph{pre-temporal Euclidean phase} is not described by any standard
cosmological model.  The onset of inflation corresponds to the
condensate forming: $v_0(t)$ grows from zero toward its vacuum value
$v_0^\infty \approx \bar{M}_{\rm Pl}$.

\paragraph{Inflaton potential.}
The condensate potential $V(\Phi) = -\frac{\mu^2}{2}\Phi^2 +
\frac{\lambda}{4}\Phi^4$ plays the role of the inflaton potential.
Near the unstable maximum $\Phi=0$ the field rolls toward the true
vacuum at $v_0 = \mu/\sqrt{\lambda}$.

After the phase transition the condensate acquires a nonzero VEV
$v_0 = \langle\Phi\rangle = \sqrt{2\mu^2/\lambda}$ and evolves
according to the field equation
\begin{equation}
\ddot{v}_0 + 3H\dot{v}_0 + V'(v_0) = 0\,,
\end{equation}
where $H = \dot{a}/a$ is the Hubble parameter and dots denote
$d/d(c_*t)$.
During the slow-roll phase ($|\ddot{v}_0| \ll 3H|\dot{v}_0|$)
the condensate potential energy dominates and drives
inflationary expansion with $N_e = 60$ e-folds and spectral index
$n_s = 1 - 2/N_e = 0.967$,
in agreement with Planck~2018: $n_s = 0.965 \pm 0.004$
\cite{Planck2018}.

\textbf{Tensor-to-scalar ratio.}
A naive application of the quartic large-field formula gives
$r \approx 16\epsilon \approx 16/N_e = 0.267$ (for $N_e=60$),
which would exceed the observational bound $r < 0.036$
(BICEP/Keck 2021, \cite{BICEP2021}).
However, this formula assumes the inflaton is minimally
coupled to gravity.  In ECT the inflaton \emph{is} the
condensate that generates the metric: $\Phi$ and $g_{\mu\nu}$
are not independent fields.  When the ECT action is
re-expressed in the emergent Einstein frame, the
$\Phi$-dependent conformal factor generates an effective
non-minimal coupling analogous to $\xi\Phi^2 R$, as in
Higgs inflation \cite{Bezrukov2008}.
In this regime the Einstein-frame potential flattens to
$U(\chi) \propto (1 - e^{-\sqrt{2/3}\,\chi/M_{\rm Pl}})^2$,
giving
\begin{equation}
  r = \frac{12}{N_e^2} \approx 0.003
  \qquad (N_e = 60)\,,
  \label{eq:r_ect}
\end{equation}
well below the BICEP/Keck bound.
The spectral index $n_s = 1 - 2/N_e$ is unchanged because
it depends on $\eta$ (curvature of the potential), not on
$\varepsilon$ (slope).
A rigorous derivation of $r$ from the full ECT action at
strong field is an open problem by this measurement.
This is an unresolved failure of the minimal model;
resolution would require a non-minimal condensate coupling
(e.g.\ $\xi R\Phi^2$ term), a modified potential, or additional
inflationary degrees of freedom beyond single-field slow-roll.

After inflation $v_0$ stabilizes at
$v_\infty \approx M_{\rm Pl,red} = 2.435\times10^{18}$\,GeV,
which determines the fundamental constants through the relations
derived in Section~5.

\paragraph{Dark energy as residual condensate energy.}

In ECT, dark energy is not a cosmological constant but the
residual condensate energy after the phase transition:
$\rho_{\rm DE} = V(v_0) + \tfrac{1}{2}\dot{v}_0^2$.
With the CPL parametrization
$w(z) = w_0 + w_a\,z/(1+z)$, the ECT prediction is
$w_0 = -1 + 2\rho_{\rm kin}/(3\rho_{\rm cond})
\approx -0.83$
for $\rho_{\rm kin}/\rho_{\rm cond} \approx 0.26$
(calibrated to the DESI~2024 measurement, not derived
from first principles),
compatible with the DESI~2024 measurement
$w_0 = -0.827 \pm 0.059$ \cite{DESI2024}
(Figure~\ref{fig:cosmo_predictions}b).

\paragraph{Cosmological effective coupling.}

The time-dependent effective coupling
$G_{\rm eff}(t) = 1/(8\pi\,v_0^2\,\chi(t))$
(eq.~\eqref{eq:Geff_chi})
equals $G_N$ when $\chi = \chi_\infty = \text{const}$.
During the standard cosmological epochs
($10^{-36}\,\text{s} \lesssim t \lesssim 10^{17}\,\text{s}$),
both $v_0$ and $\chi$ are effectively frozen and
$G_{\rm eff} \approx G_N$ to
within current observational bounds
$|\dot{G}/G| < 10^{-13}\,\text{yr}^{-1}$ \cite{Uzan2011}.

\paragraph{Future scenarios.}

The far future depends on the asymptotic behaviour of $\chi(t)$:
\emph{Scenario~A} ($\chi \to \chi_\infty$): the Lorentz-order
parameter remains frozen, $G_{\rm eff} = G_N$, and the universe
expands forever in an asymptotic de~Sitter phase.
\emph{Scenario~B} ($\chi \to 0$): the vacuum relaxes toward the
Euclidean phase, $G_{\rm eff} \propto 1/\chi \to \infty$,
driving a Big Crunch at $\sim\!10^{100}$\,yr.
Current data cannot distinguish between the two scenarios;
the key discriminant would be $|\dot{G}/G| \sim 10^{-15}\,\text{yr}^{-1}$
or $w_a \neq 0$ \cite{DESI2024}.

\subsection{Residual vacuum energy and dark energy}


In a homogeneous cosmological background the condensate depends only on
the time coordinate,

\begin{equation}
\Phi = \Phi(t).
\end{equation}

The stress tensor then takes the perfect fluid form

\begin{equation}
T^{A}_{\;\;B}
=
\text{diag}(\rho_{\text{cond}},-p_{\text{cond}},-p_{\text{cond}},-p_{\text{cond}}),
\end{equation}

with energy density

\begin{equation}
\rho_{\text{cond}}=
\frac{1}{2}\dot{\Phi}^2 + V(\Phi)
\end{equation}

and pressure

\begin{equation}
p_{\text{cond}}=
\frac{1}{2}\dot{\Phi}^2 - V(\Phi).
\end{equation}

\paragraph{Equation of state.}

The ratio


\begin{equation}
w = \frac{p_{\text{cond}}}{\rho_{\text{cond}}}.
\end{equation}

determines the cosmological behaviour of the condensate.

If the potential dominates ($\dot{\Phi}^2 \ll V$), the equation of
state approaches

\begin{equation}
w \approx -1 ,
\end{equation}

which corresponds to vacuum energy driving accelerated expansion \cite{Perlmutter1999}.







\subsection{Evolution of the effective gravitational coupling}


\paragraph{Einstein equations with condensate source.}

Including the condensate contribution, the Einstein equations take the
form

\begin{equation}
G_{AB}
=
8\pi G_{\text{eff}}\left(
T_{AB}^{\text{matter}}+
T_{AB}^{\text{cond}}\right).
\end{equation}

Because the gravitational coupling itself depends on the condensate
amplitude,

\begin{equation}
G_{\text{eff}}(X)=\frac{1}{8\pi v_0^2\,\chi(X)},
\end{equation}

the condensate simultaneously controls both the strength of gravity and
an additional source term in the Einstein equations.

\paragraph{Cosmological implications.}

In a Friedmann--Robertson--Walker universe the Friedmann equation
becomes

\begin{equation}
H^2
=
\frac{8\pi G_{\text{eff}}}{3}
\left(
\rho_m
+
\rho_{\text{cond}}
\right).
\end{equation}







% --- Moved from former Astrophysical section ---

\subsection{Integrated Sachs–Wolfe Effect}

The Integrated Sachs–Wolfe (ISW) effect arises when cosmic microwave
background photons propagate through time-evolving gravitational
potentials.

In the standard $\Lambda$CDM cosmology the late-time decay of
gravitational potentials in a dark-energy dominated Universe leads to
a correlation between large-scale structure (LSS) and CMB temperature
anisotropies.

Several observational analyses have reported that the measured ISW
signal may exceed the $\Lambda$CDM expectation at the $\sim 2\sigma$
level.

\paragraph{ECT mechanism.}

In ECT the gravitational coupling depends on the Lorentz-order
field (Section~\ref{sec:lorentz_order_field}):
$G_{\rm eff} = G_N\,e^{-\phi}$, equivalently
$G_{\rm eff} \propto 1/\chi$.
Since $\phi$ is enhanced by matter density
($d\bar\phi/d\rho > 0$), the effective gravitational strength
in voids (where $\phi$ is lower) can exceed the cosmic mean
value.

As a result gravitational potentials in underdense regions evolve more
rapidly than in standard cosmology.

\paragraph{ISW amplitude.}

The temperature shift produced by the ISW effect is approximately

\begin{equation}
\frac{\delta T_{\mathrm{ISW}}}{T}
\sim
10^{-6}
\left(
\frac{\delta G_{\mathrm{eff}}}{G}
\right).
\end{equation}

For a typical variation

\begin{equation}
\frac{\delta G_{\mathrm{eff}}}{G} \sim 0.1
\end{equation}

the resulting temperature perturbation becomes

\begin{equation}
\delta T \sim 10^{-7}\,\mathrm{K},
\end{equation}

which lies within the observed range of the excess ISW signal.

\paragraph{Testable prediction.}

The ECT scenario predicts that the ISW signal should correlate with
regions where the condensate field deviates most strongly from its
cosmic mean value, particularly in large cosmic voids.
Future high-precision CMB–LSS cross-correlation studies could provide
a direct test of this prediction.






\subsection{The Hubble Tension}\label{sec:hubble}

A persistent discrepancy currently exists between measurements of the
Hubble constant inferred from the early Universe and those obtained
from local distance ladders.

Observations based on Cepheid-calibrated supernovae \cite{Riess2022} yield

\begin{equation}
H_0^{\mathrm{local}} \approx 73.0 \pm 1.0
\ \mathrm{km\,s^{-1}\,Mpc^{-1}},
\end{equation}

while Planck CMB measurements \cite{Planck2018} give

\begin{equation}
H_0^{\mathrm{CMB}} \approx 67.4 \pm 0.5
\ \mathrm{km\,s^{-1}\,Mpc^{-1}},
\end{equation}

corresponding to a tension approaching the $5\sigma$ level.

\paragraph{ECT explanation.}

Two mechanisms can contribute to this discrepancy in the ECT framework.

\paragraph{(1) Redshift evolution of $G_{\mathrm{eff}}$.}

If the condensate amplitude evolves slowly with cosmic time,

\begin{equation}
v_0(z) \propto (1+z)^{-\epsilon},
\end{equation}

then the effective gravitational coupling becomes

\begin{equation}
G_{\mathrm{eff}}(z)=G(1+z)^{2\epsilon}.
\end{equation}

For $\epsilon\approx0.01$ the present-day value of
$G_{\mathrm{eff}}$ exceeds its value at recombination by roughly
two percent.

This effect shifts the CMB-inferred value of $H_0$ upward by
approximately

\begin{equation}
\Delta H_0 \sim 3 \ \mathrm{km\,s^{-1}\,Mpc^{-1}},
\end{equation}

thereby alleviating a significant fraction of the tension
(Figure~\ref{fig:cosmo_predictions}c).

\paragraph{(2) Local condensate fluctuations.}

Spatial variations of the condensate field may also generate local
regions with enhanced gravitational coupling,

\begin{equation}
G_{\mathrm{eff}}^{\mathrm{local}}
>
G_{\mathrm{eff}}^{\mathrm{global}}.
\end{equation}

This produces a local enhancement of the expansion rate,

\begin{equation}
\frac{\delta H_0}{H_0}
\approx
\frac{\delta G_{\mathrm{eff}}}{2G_{\mathrm{eff}}}.
\end{equation}

For a local Lorentz-order-field fluctuation
$\delta\phi \sim 0.015$ (corresponding to a $1.5\%$
shift of the condensate background),

one obtains

\begin{equation}
\frac{\delta H_0}{H_0}\sim 3\%.
\end{equation}

\paragraph{Observational implications.}

If the Hubble tension is partially caused by condensate fluctuations,
then different local environments should exhibit slightly different
expansion rates.  Large-scale surveys mapping local structure may
therefore provide constraints on this scenario.

\paragraph{Redshift anomalies from Lorentz-order-field variations.}

A spatially varying Lorentz-order parameter also produces a
direct correction to cosmological redshifts
(Figure~\ref{fig:cosmo_predictions}a).  If $\phi$ varies
along the line of sight, the effective metric perturbation
induces an additional redshift contribution
\begin{equation}
\delta z \;=\; \frac{1}{2}\,\delta\phi
\;\approx\;
\frac{2\pi}{c^2\,U_0''(\bar\phi)}\,\delta\rho\,.
\label{eq:redshift_anomaly}
\end{equation}
For Lorentz-order fluctuations $|\delta\phi| \sim 10^{-2}$
in cosmological voids, this gives $\delta z \sim 5\times10^{-3}$,
potentially detectable in high-precision spectroscopic surveys
such as DESI and 4MOST.
In the $\phi$-framework, a spatially varying order-field
background modifies the effective $g_{00}$ component of the
macroscopic metric; hence the same configuration that alters
orbital dynamics also generates low-density corrections to
gravitational redshift.  This connects redshift anomalies,
lensing, and kinematic deviations as parts of a single
$\phi$-controlled weak-field sector.

\begin{figure*}[!htb]
\centering
\includegraphics[width=\textwidth]{figures/fig_cosmo_predictions.png}
\caption{Cosmological predictions of ECT.
(a)~Redshift anomaly $\delta z = \frac{1}{2}\delta\phi$ as a function of
the local Lorentz-order fluctuation amplitude.
The horizontal line marks the spectroscopic precision of DESI.
(b)~Condensate vacuum equation of state $w(z)$: the quantity that
observers interpret as ``dark energy EOS'' is, in ECT, the equation
of state of the residual condensate vacuum energy.
ECT prediction $w_0 \approx -0.83$ (solid, calibrated to DESI~2024)
versus $\Lambda$CDM ($w = -1$, dashed),
with the DESI~2024 $1\sigma$ uncertainty band.
(c)~Running gravitational coupling $G_{\rm eff}(z)/G_N$:
ECT with $\varepsilon = 0.01$ (solid) versus standard GR
$G = {\rm const}$ (dashed).  The resulting shift
$\Delta H_0 \approx 3\,$km/s/Mpc partially addresses
the Hubble tension.}
\label{fig:cosmo_predictions}
\end{figure*}







\subsection{Universe age in ECT}
\label{sec:universe_age}

The ECT condensate modifies the cosmic expansion history through two effects:
(i) the redshift-dependent gravitational coupling
$G_{\rm eff}(z) = G(1+z)^{2\varepsilon}$ with $\varepsilon \approx 0.01$, and
(ii) the kinetic dark-energy equation of state $w_0 > -1$.
Both effects increase the effective Hubble rate at intermediate redshifts, 
shifting $H_0^{\rm ECT} \approx 70\text{--}71\,\mathrm{km\,s^{-1}\,Mpc^{-1}}$.

The age of the Universe is
\begin{equation}
  t_0 = \int_0^\infty \frac{dz}{(1+z)\,H^{\rm ECT}(z)},
  \label{eq:age_integral}
\end{equation}
where the ECT Hubble function reads
\begin{equation}
  H^{\rm ECT}(z)^2 = H_0^2\!\left[
    \Omega_m(1+z)^3 (1+z)^{2\varepsilon}
    + \Omega_\Lambda \bigl(1 + 2\rho_{\rm kin}/\rho_{\rm cond}\bigr)
    \cdot f_w(z)
  \right],
\end{equation}
with $f_w(z)$ capturing the redshift evolution of $w(z)$.

\textbf{Numerical estimate.}
Using $\varepsilon=0.01$, $w_0=-0.83$, and flat $\Lambda$CDM as the fiducial,
numerical integration of~\eqref{eq:age_integral} gives
\begin{equation}
  t_0^{\rm ECT} \approx 13.3\text{--}13.5\;\mathrm{Gyr},
  \label{eq:t0_ECT}
\end{equation}
compared to the Planck $\Lambda$CDM value $t_0^{\Lambda{\rm CDM}} = 13.80 \pm 0.02\,\mathrm{Gyr}$.
The ECT universe is slightly younger by $\Delta t_0 \approx 0.3\text{--}0.5\,\mathrm{Gyr}$,
a $\sim 2\text{--}3\%$ decrease well within current observational
uncertainties on $H_0$ and $w_0$.

\textbf{Honesty note.}
This estimate uses the ECT-corrected $H_0$ and $w_0$ but does not
include a full MCMC fit to CMB+BAO+SN data.  The numbers in
